   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Recycling
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <div class="flaggedrevs_short flaggedrevs_stable_synced plainlinks noprint nomobile" id="mw-fr-revisiontag">
      <div class="flaggedrevs_short_basic">
       <img alt="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page." class="flaggedrevs-icon" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/doc-magnify.png" title="Changes must be reviewed before being displayed on this page."/>
       <img alt="show/hide details" class="fr-toggle-arrow" id="mw-fr-revisiontoggle" src="/w/extensions/FlaggedRevs/frontend/modules/img/arrow-down.png" style="display:none;"/>
      </div>
      <div id="mw-fr-revisiondetails-wrapper" style="position:relative;">
       <div class="flaggedrevs_short_details" id="mw-fr-revisiondetails" style="display:none">
        This is the
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Pending_changes" title="Wikipedia:Pending changes">
         latest accepted revision
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Special:Log&amp;type=review&amp;page=Recycling">
         reviewed
        </a>
        on
        <i>
         4 November 2016
        </i>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Recycling_symbol.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="614" data-file-width="628" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Recycling_symbol.svg/220px-Recycling_symbol.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Recycling_symbol.svg/330px-Recycling_symbol.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Recycling_symbol.svg/440px-Recycling_symbol.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Recycling_symbol.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The three chasing arrows of the international recycling logo. It is sometimes accompanied by the text "reduce, reuse and recycle".
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Recycling
      </b>
      is the process of converting
      <a href="/wiki/Waste" title="Waste">
       waste
      </a>
      materials into reusable objects to prevent waste of potentially useful materials, reduce the consumption of fresh raw materials,
      <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
       energy
      </a>
      usage, air pollution (from
      <a href="/wiki/Incineration" title="Incineration">
       incineration
      </a>
      ) and water pollution (from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Landfilling" title="Landfilling">
       landfilling
      </a>
      ) by decreasing the need for "conventional" waste disposal and lowering
      <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">
       greenhouse gas
      </a>
      emissions compared to plastic production.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        [1]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recycling is a key component of modern waste reduction and is the third component of the "
      <a href="/wiki/Waste_minimisation" title="Waste minimisation">
       Reduce
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">
       Reuse
      </a>
      and Recycle"
      <a href="/wiki/Waste_hierarchy" title="Waste hierarchy">
       waste hierarchy
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      There are some
      <a href="/wiki/International_Organization_for_Standardization" title="International Organization for Standardization">
       ISO
      </a>
      standards related to recycling such as ISO 15270:2008 for plastics waste and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISO_14001" title="ISO 14001">
       ISO 14001
      </a>
      :2004 for environmental management control of recycling practice.
     </p>
     <p>
      Recyclable materials include many kinds of glass, paper and cardboard, metal, plastic,
      <a href="/wiki/Tire" title="Tire">
       tires
      </a>
      , textiles and
      <a href="/wiki/Electronics" title="Electronics">
       electronics
      </a>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Composting" title="Composting">
       composting
      </a>
      or other reuse of
      <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_waste" title="Biodegradable waste">
       biodegradable waste
      </a>
      —such as
      <a href="/wiki/Food_waste" title="Food waste">
       food
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Green_waste" title="Green waste">
       garden waste
      </a>
      —is also considered recycling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Materials to be recycled are either brought to a collection centre or picked up from the curbside, then sorted, cleaned and reprocessed into new materials destined for manufacturing.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the strictest sense, recycling of a material would produce a fresh supply of the same material—for example, used office paper would be converted into new office paper, or used
      <a href="/wiki/Polystyrene" title="Polystyrene">
       polystyrene
      </a>
      foam into new polystyrene. However, this is often difficult or too expensive (compared with producing the same product from raw materials or other sources), so "recycling" of many products or materials involves their
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Reuse" title="Reuse">
        reuse
       </a>
      </i>
      in producing different materials (for example,
      <a href="/wiki/Paperboard" title="Paperboard">
       paperboard
      </a>
      ) instead. Another form of recycling is the
      <a href="/wiki/Waste_sorting" title="Waste sorting">
       salvage
      </a>
      of certain materials from complex products, either due to their intrinsic value (such as lead from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Car_batteries" title="Car batteries">
       car batteries
      </a>
      , or gold from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circuit_board" title="Circuit board">
       circuit boards
      </a>
      ), or due to their hazardous nature (e.g., removal and reuse of
      <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">
       mercury
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Thermometer" title="Thermometer">
       thermometers
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thermostat" title="Thermostat">
       thermostats
      </a>
      ).
     </p>

         <a href="#See_also">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           12
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           See also
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-38">
         <a href="#References">
          <span class="tocnumber">
           13
          </span>
          <span class="toctext">
           References
          </span>
         </a>
        </li>
        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-39">
         <a href="#Further_reading">

          </span>

          </span>
         </a>
        </li>

          </span>

        </li>

     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins">
       Origins
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Origins">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Recycling has been a common practice for most of human history, with recorded advocates as far back as
      <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">
       Plato
      </a>
      in 400 BC. During periods when resources were scarce and hard to come by, archaeological studies of ancient waste dumps show less household waste (such as ash, broken tools and pottery)—implying more waste was being recycled in the absence of new material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guide_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-guide-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="1999" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg/220px-Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg/330px-Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg/440px-Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Scrap%5E_Will_Help_Win._Don%27t_Mix_it_-_NARA_-_533983.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An American poster from
        <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
         World War II
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pre-industrial" title="Pre-industrial">
       pre-industrial
      </a>
      times, there is evidence of scrap bronze and other metals being collected in Europe and melted down for perpetual reuse.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Paper recycling was first recorded in 1031, when Japanese shops sold repulped paper.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In Britain dust and ash from wood and coal fires was collected by "
      <a href="/wiki/Waste_collector" title="Waste collector">
       dustmen
      </a>
      " and
      <a href="/wiki/Downcycling" title="Downcycling">
       downcycled
      </a>
      as a base material used in brick making. The main driver for these types of recycling was the economic advantage of obtaining recycled feedstock instead of acquiring virgin material, as well as a lack of public waste removal in ever more densely populated areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guide_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-guide-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1813,
      <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Law_(inventor)" title="Benjamin Law (inventor)">
       Benjamin Law
      </a>
      developed the process of turning rags into "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shoddy" title="Shoddy">
       shoddy
      </a>
      " and "
      <a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_textile_manufacturing#M" title="Glossary of textile manufacturing">
       mungo
      </a>
      " wool in Batley, Yorkshire. This material combined recycled fibers with virgin
      <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">
       wool
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">
       West Yorkshire
      </a>
      shoddy industry in towns such as Batley and Dewsbury, lasted from the early 19th century to at least 1914.
     </p>
     <p>
      Industrialization spurred demand for affordable materials; aside from rags, ferrous
      <a href="/wiki/Scrap" title="Scrap">
       scrap
      </a>
      metals were coveted as they were cheaper to acquire than virgin ore. Railroads both purchased and sold scrap metal in the 19th century, and the growing steel and automobile industries purchased scrap in the early 20th century. Many secondary goods were collected, processed and sold by peddlers who scoured dumps and city streets for discarded machinery, pots, pans and other sources of metal. By
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">
       World War I
      </a>
      , thousands of such peddlers roamed the streets of American cities, taking advantage of market forces to recycle post-consumer materials back into industrial production.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cash_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cash-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Beverage bottles were recycled with a refundable deposit at some drink manufacturers in Great Britain and Ireland around 1800, notably
      <a href="/wiki/Schweppes" title="Schweppes">
       Schweppes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An official recycling system with refundable
      <a href="/wiki/Container_deposit_legislation" title="Container deposit legislation">
       deposits
      </a>
      was established in Sweden for bottles in 1884 and aluminum beverage cans in 1982; the law led to a recycling rate for beverage containers of 84–99 percent depending on type, and a glass bottle can be refilled over 20 times on average.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper,_rags,_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3520" data-file-width="2492" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg/220px-INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg/330px-INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg/440px-INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper%2C_rags%2C_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:INF3-196_Salvage_Still_more_paper,_rags,_bones_wanted_for_salvage_Artist_Gilroy.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        British poster from World War II
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Wartime">
       Wartime
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Wartime">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      New chemical industries created in the late 19th century both invented new materials (e.g.
      <a href="/wiki/Bakelite" title="Bakelite">
       Bakelite
      </a>
      (1907) and promised to transform valueless into valuable materials. Proverbially, you could not
      <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/make_a_silk_purse_of_a_sow%27s_ear" title="wikt:make a silk purse of a sow's ear">
       make a silk purse of a sow's ear
      </a>
      —until the US firm Arhur D. Little published in 1921 "On the Making of Silk Purses from Sows' Ears", its research proving that when "chemistry puts on overalls and gets down to business . . .new values appear. New and better paths are opened to reach the goals desired."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recycling was a highlight throughout World War II. During the war, financial constraints and significant material shortages due to war efforts made it necessary for countries to reuse goods and recycle materials.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-PBS-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      These resource shortages caused by the
      <a href="/wiki/World_war" title="World war">
       world wars
      </a>
      , and other such world-changing occurrences, greatly encouraged recycling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The struggles of war claimed much of the material resources available, leaving little for the civilian population.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-PBS-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It became necessary for most homes to recycle their waste, as recycling offered an extra source of materials allowing people to make the most of what was available to them. Recycling household materials meant more resources for war efforts and a better chance of victory.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PBS_10-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-PBS-10">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Massive government promotion campaigns were carried out in the
      <a href="/wiki/Home_front_during_World_War_II" title="Home front during World War II">
       home front during World War II
      </a>
      in every country involved in the war, urging citizens to donate metals and conserve fiber, as a matter of patriotism.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Post-war">
       Post-war
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Post-war">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A considerable investment in recycling occurred in the 1970s, due to rising energy costs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Recycling aluminum uses only 5% of the energy required by virgin production; glass, paper and metals have less dramatic but very significant energy savings when recycled feedstock is used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economistrecycle_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-economistrecycle-13">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although consumer electronics such as the television have been popular since the 1920s, recycling of them was almost unheard of until early 1991.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The first electronic waste recycling scheme was implemented in Switzerland, beginning with collection of old refrigerators but gradually expanding to cover all devices.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After these schemes were set up, many countries did not have the capacity to deal with the sheer quantity of e-waste they generated or its hazardous nature. They began to export the problem to developing countries without enforced environmental legislation. This is cheaper, as recycling computer monitors in the United States costs 10 times more than in China. Demand in Asia for electronic waste began to grow when scrap yards found that they could extract valuable substances such as copper, silver, iron, silicon, nickel and gold, during the recycling process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 2000s saw a large increase in both the sale of electronic devices and their growth as a waste stream: in 2002, e-waste grew faster than any other type of waste in the EU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kinver_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kinver-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      This caused investment in modern, automated facilities to cope with the influx of redundant appliances, especially after strict laws were implemented in 2003.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As of 2014, the
      <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">
       European Union
      </a>
      has about 50% of world share of the waste and recycling industries, with over 60,000 companies employing 500,000 persons, with a turnover of €24 billion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Countries have to reach recycling rates of at least 50%, while the lead countries are around 65% and the EU average is 39% as of 2013.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation">
       Legislation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Legislation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Supply">
       Supply
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Supply">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      For a recycling program to work, having a large, stable
      <a href="/wiki/Supply_and_demand" title="Supply and demand">
       supply
      </a>
      of recyclable material is crucial. Three legislative options have been used to create such a supply: mandatory recycling collection,
      <a href="/wiki/Container_deposit_legislation" title="Container deposit legislation">
       container deposit legislation
      </a>
      and refuse bans. Mandatory collection laws set recycling targets for cities to aim for, usually in the form that a certain percentage of a material must be diverted from the city's waste stream by a target date. The city is then responsible for working to meet this target.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Container deposit legislation involves offering a refund for the return of certain containers, typically glass, plastic and metal. When a product in such a container is purchased, a small surcharge is added to the price. This surcharge can be reclaimed by the consumer if the container is returned to a collection point. These programs have been very successful, often resulting in an 80 percent recycling rate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite such good results, the shift in collection costs from local government to industry and consumers has created strong opposition to the creation of such programs in some areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A variation on this is where the manufacturer bears responsibility for the recycling of their goods. In the European Union, the WEEE Directive requires producers of consumer electronics to reimburse the recyclers' costs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An alternative way to increase supply of recyclates is to
      <a href="/wiki/Ban_(law)" title="Ban (law)">
       ban
      </a>
      the disposal of certain materials as waste, often including used oil, old batteries, tires and garden waste. One aim of this method is to create a viable economy for proper disposal of banned products. Care must be taken that enough of these recycling
      <a href="/wiki/Service_(economics)" title="Service (economics)">
       services
      </a>
      exist, or such bans simply lead to increased
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fly-tipping" title="Fly-tipping">
       illegal dumping
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Government-mandated_demand">
       Government-mandated demand
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Government-mandated demand">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Legislation has also been used to increase and maintain a demand for recycled materials. Four methods of such legislation exist: minimum recycled content mandates, utilization rates,
      <a href="/wiki/Procurement" title="Procurement">
       procurement
      </a>
      policies and recycled
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandatory_labeling" title="Mandatory labeling">
       product labeling
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Both minimum recycled content mandates and utilization rates increase demand directly by forcing manufacturers to include recycling in their operations. Content mandates specify that a certain percentage of a new product must consist of recycled material. Utilization rates are a more flexible option: industries are permitted to meet the recycling targets at any point of their operation or even contract recycling out in exchange for tradeable credits. Opponents to both of these methods point to the large increase in reporting requirements they impose, and claim that they rob industry of necessary flexibility.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeLong_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DeLong-26">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governments" title="Governments">
       Governments
      </a>
      have used their own
      <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power" title="Purchasing power">
       purchasing power
      </a>
      to increase recycling demand through what are called "procurement policies." These policies are either "set-asides," which reserve a certain amount of spending solely towards recycled products, or "price preference" programs which provide a larger
      <a href="/wiki/Government_budget" title="Government budget">
       budget
      </a>
      when recycled items are purchased. Additional regulations can target specific cases: in the United States, for example, the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">
       Environmental Protection Agency
      </a>
      mandates the purchase of oil, paper, tires and building insulation from recycled or re-refined sources whenever possible.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The final government regulation towards increased demand is recycled product labeling. When producers are required to label their packaging with amount of recycled material in the product (including the packaging), consumers are better able to make educated choices. Consumers with sufficient
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buying_power" title="Buying power">
       buying power
      </a>
      can then choose more environmentally conscious options, prompt producers to increase the amount of recycled material in their products, and indirectly increase demand. Standardized recycling labeling can also have a positive effect on supply of recyclates if the labeling includes information on how and where the product can be recycled.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recyclates">
       Recyclates
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Recyclates">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2035" data-file-width="3000" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg/220px-CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg/330px-CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg/440px-CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CLOSEUP_OF_BEER_BOTTLES_CRUSHED_INTO_FINE_GLASS._THIS_MIXTURE_IS_FED_INTO_THE_GLASS_FURNACE_AND_NEW_BOTTLES_ARE_MADE..._-_NARA_-_543695.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Glass recovered by crushing only one kind of beer bottle
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <b>
       Recyclate
      </b>
      is a raw material that is sent to, and processed in a waste recycling plant or materials recovery facility which will be used to form new products.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The material is collected in various methods and delivered to a facility where it undergoes re-manufacturing so that it can used in the production of new materials or products. For example, plastic bottles that are collected can be re-used and made into plastic pellets, a new product.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_of_recyclate">
       Quality of recyclate
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Quality of recyclate">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The quality of recyclates is recognized as one of the principal challenges that needs to be addressed for the success of a long-term vision of a green economy and achieving zero waste. Recyclate quality is generally referring to how much of the raw material is made up of target material compared to the amount of non-target material and other non-recyclable material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Only target material is likely to be recycled, so a higher amount of non-target and non-recyclable material will reduce the quantity of recycling product.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A high proportion of non-target and non-recyclable material can make it more difficult for re-processors to achieve "high-quality" recycling. If the recyclate is of poor quality, it is more likely to end up being down-cycled or, in more extreme cases, sent to other recovery options or landfilled.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, to facilitate the re-manufacturing of clear glass products there are tight restrictions for colored glass going into the re-melt process.
     </p>
     <p>
      The quality of recyclate not only supports high quality recycling, but it can also deliver significant environmental benefits by reducing, reusing and keeping products out of landfills.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      High quality recycling can help support growth in the economy by maximizing the economic value of the waste material collected.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Higher income levels from the sale of quality recyclates can return value which can be significant to local governments, households and businesses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GOV.UK_29-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-GOV.UK-29">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pursuing high quality recycling can also provide consumer and business confidence in the waste and resource management sector and may encourage investment in that sector.
     </p>
     <p>
      There are many actions along the recycling supply chain that can influence and affect the material quality of recyclate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It begins with the waste producers who place non-target and non-recyclable wastes in recycling collection. This can affect the quality of final recyclate streams or require further efforts to discard those materials at later stages in the recycling process.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The different collection systems can result in different levels of contamination. Depending on which materials are collected together, extra effort is required to sort this material back into separate streams and can significantly reduce the quality of the final product.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Transportation and the compaction of materials can make it more difficult to separate material back into separate waste streams. Sorting facilities are not one hundred per cent effective in separating materials, despite improvements in technology and quality recyclate which can see a loss in recyclate quality.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The storage of materials outside where the product can become wet can cause problems for re-processors. Reprocessing facilities may require further sorting steps to further reduce the amount of non-target and non-recyclable material.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Each action along the recycling path plays a part in the quality of recyclate.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Quality_recyclate_action_plan_.28Scotland.29">
       Quality recyclate action plan (Scotland)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Quality recyclate action plan (Scotland)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The Recyclate Quality Action Plan of Scotland sets out a number of proposed actions that the Scottish Government would like to take forward in order to drive up the quality of the materials being collected for recycling and sorted at materials recovery facilities before being exported or sold on to the reprocessing market.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The plan's objectives are to:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highland_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-highland-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Drive up the quality of recyclate.
      </li>
      <li>
       Deliver greater transparency about the quality of recyclate.
      </li>
      <li>
       Provide help to those contracting with materials recycling facilities to identify what is required of them
      </li>
      <li>
       Ensure compliance with the Waste (Scotland) regulations 2012.
      </li>
      <li>
       Stimulate a household market for quality recyclate.
      </li>
      <li>
       Address and reduce issues surrounding the Waste Shipment Regulations.
      </li>
     </ul>
     <p>
      The plan focuses on three key areas, with fourteen actions which were identified to increase the quality of materials collected, sorted and presented to the processing market in Scotland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-highland_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-highland-31">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The three areas of focus are:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Scottish_30-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-Scottish-30">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ol>
      <li>
       Collection systems and input contamination
      </li>
      <li>
       Sorting facilities – material sampling and transparency
      </li>
      <li>
       Material quality benchmarking and standards
      </li>
     </ol>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling_consumer_waste">
       Recycling consumer waste
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Recycling consumer waste">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Collection">
       Collection
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Collection">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="967" data-file-width="644" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg/220px-DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg/330px-DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg/440px-DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DeutscheBahnRecycling20050814_CopyrightKaihsuTai_Rotated.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A three-sided bin at a railway station in Germany, intended to separate paper
        <i>
         (left)
        </i>
        and plastic wrappings
        <i>
         (right)
        </i>
        from other waste
        <i>
         (back)
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A number of different systems have been implemented to collect recyclates from the general waste stream. These systems lie along the spectrum of trade-off between public convenience and government ease and expense. The three main categories of collection are "drop-off centers," "buy-back centers" and "curbside collection."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Curbside_collection">
       Curbside collection
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Curbside collection">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Curbside_collection" title="Curbside collection">
       Curbside collection
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Curbside collection encompasses many subtly different systems, which differ mostly on where in the process the recyclates are sorted and cleaned. The main categories are mixed waste collection, commingled recyclables and source separation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Waste_collection_vehicle" title="Waste collection vehicle">
       waste collection vehicle
      </a>
      generally picks up the waste.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ACT_recycling_truck.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ACT_recycling_truck.jpg/220px-ACT_recycling_truck.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ACT_recycling_truck.jpg/330px-ACT_recycling_truck.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/ACT_recycling_truck.jpg/440px-ACT_recycling_truck.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ACT_recycling_truck.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A recycling truck collecting the contents of a
        <a href="/wiki/Recycling_bin" title="Recycling bin">
         recycling bin
        </a>
        in
        <a href="/wiki/Canberra" title="Canberra">
         Canberra
        </a>
        , Australia
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      At one end of the spectrum is mixed waste collection, in which all recyclates are collected mixed in with the rest of the waste, and the desired material is then sorted out and cleaned at a central sorting facility. This results in a large amount of recyclable waste, paper especially, being too soiled to reprocess, but has advantages as well: the city need not pay for a separate collection of recyclates and no public education is needed. Any changes to which materials are recyclable is easy to accommodate as all sorting happens in a central location.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In a commingled or
      <a href="/wiki/Single-stream_recycling" title="Single-stream recycling">
       single-stream system
      </a>
      , all recyclables for collection are mixed but kept separate from other waste. This greatly reduces the need for post-collection cleaning but does require
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_education" title="Public education">
       public education
      </a>
      on what materials are recyclable.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Source separation is the other extreme, where each material is cleaned and sorted prior to collection. This method requires the least post-collection sorting and produces the purest recyclates, but incurs additional
      <a href="/wiki/Operating_cost" title="Operating cost">
       operating costs
      </a>
      for collection of each separate material. An extensive public education program is also required, which must be successful if recyclate contamination is to be avoided.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Source separation used to be the preferred method due to the high sorting costs incurred by commingled (mixed waste) collection. Advances in sorting technology (see
      <a href="/wiki/Recycling#Sorting" title="Recycling">
       sorting
      </a>
      below), however, have lowered this overhead substantially—many areas which had developed source separation programs have since switched to co-mingled collection.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Buy-back_centers">
       Buy-back centers
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Buy-back centers">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Buy-back centers differ in that the cleaned recyclates are purchased, thus providing a clear incentive for use and creating a stable supply. The post-processed material can then be sold on, hopefully creating a profit. Unfortunately, government subsidies are necessary to make buy-back centres a viable enterprise, as according to the U.S.
      <a href="/wiki/National_Waste_%26_Recycling_Association" title="National Waste &amp; Recycling Association">
       National Waste &amp; Recycling Association
      </a>
      , it costs on average US$50 to process a ton of material, which can only be resold for US$30.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
        [2]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Drop-off_centers">
       Drop-off centers
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Drop-off centers">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Drop-off centers require the waste producer to carry the recyclates to a central location, either an installed or mobile collection station or the reprocessing plant itself. They are the easiest type of collection to establish, but suffer from low and unpredictable throughput.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Distributed_Recycling">
       Distributed Recycling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Distributed Recycling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      For some waste materials such as plastic, recent technical devices called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recyclebots" title="Recyclebots">
       recyclebots
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RPJ_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RPJ-32">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      enable a form of distributed recycling. Preliminary
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Life-cycle_analysis" title="Life-cycle analysis">
       life-cycle analysis
      </a>
      (LCA) indicates that such distributed recycling of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HDPE" title="HDPE">
       HDPE
      </a>
      to make filament of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/3-D_printers" title="3-D printers">
       3-D printers
      </a>
      in rural regions is energetically favorable to either using virgin resin or conventional recycling processes because of reductions in transportation energy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LCA_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LCA-33">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sorting">
       Sorting
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sorting">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
