        <img alt="File:Recycling Video.webm" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Recycling_Video.webm/220px--Recycling_Video.webm.jpg" style="width:220px;height:124px"/>
        <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d4/Recycling_Video.webm" target="new" title="Play media">
         <span class="play-btn-large">
          <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
           Play media
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Recycling_Video.webm" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Recycling sorting facility and processes
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Once commingled recyclates are collected and delivered to a
      <a href="/wiki/Materials_recovery_facility" title="Materials recovery facility">
       central collection facility
      </a>
      , the different types of materials must be sorted. This is done in a series of stages, many of which involve automated processes such that a truckload of material can be fully sorted in less than an hour.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some plants can now sort the materials automatically, known as
      <a href="/wiki/Single-stream_recycling" title="Single-stream recycling">
       single-stream recycling
      </a>
      . In plants, a variety of materials are sorted such as paper, different types of plastics, glass, metals, food scraps and most types of batteries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 30 percent increase in recycling rates has been seen in the areas where these plants exist.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Initially, the commingled recyclates are removed from the collection vehicle and placed on a conveyor belt spread out in a single layer. Large pieces of
      <a href="/wiki/Corrugated_fiberboard" title="Corrugated fiberboard">
       corrugated fiberboard
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Plastic_bag" title="Plastic bag">
       plastic bags
      </a>
      are removed by hand at this stage, as they can cause later machinery to jam.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1564" data-file-width="2195" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG/220px-Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG/330px-Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG/440px-Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Glass_and_plastic_recycling_065_ubt.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Early sorting of recyclable materials: glass and plastic bottles in
        <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">
         Poland
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Next, automated machinery such as disk screens and air classifiers separate the recyclates by weight, splitting lighter paper and plastic from heavier glass and metal. Cardboard is removed from the mixed paper and the most common types of plastic,
      <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">
       PET
      </a>
      (#1) and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HDPE" title="HDPE">
       HDPE
      </a>
      (#2), are collected. This separation is usually done by hand but has become automated in some sorting centers: a
      <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">
       spectroscopic
      </a>
      scanner is used to differentiate between different types of paper and plastic based on the absorbed wavelengths, and subsequently divert each material into the proper collection channel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Strong magnets are used to separate out
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferrous_metal" title="Ferrous metal">
       ferrous metals
      </a>
      , such as iron, steel and
      <a href="/wiki/Tin_can" title="Tin can">
       tin cans
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Non-ferrous_metal" title="Non-ferrous metal">
       Non-ferrous metals
      </a>
      are ejected by magnetic
      <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">
       eddy currents
      </a>
      in which a rotating
      <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
       magnetic field
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_induction" title="Electromagnetic induction">
       induces
      </a>
      an electric current around the aluminum cans, which in turn creates a magnetic eddy current inside the cans. This magnetic
      <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">
       eddy current
      </a>
      is repulsed by a large magnetic field, and the cans are ejected from the rest of the recyclate stream.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="640" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg/220px-Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg/330px-Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg/440px-Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Recycling_Point_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1382457.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A recycling point in
        <a href="/wiki/New_Byth" title="New Byth">
         New Byth
        </a>
        , Scotland, with separate containers for paper, plastics and differently colored glass
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Finally, glass is sorted according to its color: brown, amber, green or clear. It may either be sorted by hand,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economisttruth_4-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-economisttruth-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      or via an automated machine that uses colored filters to detect different colors. Glass fragments smaller than 10 millimetres (0.39 in) across cannot be sorted automatically, and are mixed together as "glass fines."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      This process of recycling as well as reusing the recycled material has proven advantageous because it reduces amount of waste sent to landfills, conserves natural resources, saves energy, reduces greenhouse gas emissions and helps create new jobs. Recycled materials can also be converted into new products that can be consumed again, such as paper, plastic and glass.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The City and County of San Francisco's Department of the Environment is attempting to achieve a citywide goal of
      <a class="external text" href="http://www.sfenvironment.org/zero-waste/overview/goals" rel="nofollow">
       Zero Waste by 2020
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
      San Francisco's refuse hauler,
      <a href="/wiki/Recology" title="Recology">
       Recology
      </a>
      , operates an effective recyclables sorting facility in San Francisco, which helped San Francisco reach a record-breaking diversion rate of 80%.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rinsing">
       Rinsing
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Rinsing">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Food_packaging" title="Food packaging">
       Food packaging
      </a>
      should no longer contain any organic matter (organic matter, if any, needs to be placed in a
      <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_waste" title="Biodegradable waste">
       biodegradable waste bin
      </a>
      or be buried in a garden
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). Since no trace of biodegradable material is best kept in the packaging before placing it in a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trash_bag" title="Trash bag">
       trash bag
      </a>
      , some packaging also needs to be rinsed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling_industrial_waste">
       Recycling industrial waste
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Recycling industrial waste">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shredded_tires.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shredded_tires.JPG/220px-Shredded_tires.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shredded_tires.JPG/330px-Shredded_tires.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Shredded_tires.JPG/440px-Shredded_tires.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shredded_tires.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Mounds of shredded rubber tires are ready for processing
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Although many government programs are concentrated on recycling at home, a 64% of waste in the United Kingdom is generated by industry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The focus of many recycling programs done by industry is the cost–effectiveness of recycling. The ubiquitous nature of cardboard packaging makes cardboard a commonly recycled waste product by companies that deal heavily in packaged goods, like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retail_store" title="Retail store">
       retail stores
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Warehouse" title="Warehouse">
       warehouses
      </a>
      and distributors of goods. Other industries deal in niche or specialized products, depending on the nature of the waste materials that are present.
     </p>
     <p>
      The glass, lumber,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pulp" title="Wood pulp">
       wood pulp
      </a>
      and paper manufacturers all deal directly in commonly recycled materials; however, old
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rubber_tires" title="Rubber tires">
       rubber tires
      </a>
      may be collected and recycled by independent tire dealers for a profit.
     </p>
     <p>
      Levels of metals recycling are generally low. In 2010, the
      <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">
       International Resource Panel
      </a>
      , hosted by the
      <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">
       United Nations Environment Programme
      </a>
      (UNEP) published reports on metal stocks that exist within society
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRP_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-IRP-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and their recycling rates.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IRP_44-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-IRP-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Panel reported that the increase in the use of metals during the 20th and into the 21st century has led to a substantial shift in metal stocks from below ground to use in applications within society above ground. For example, the in-use stock of copper in the USA grew from 73 to 238 kg per capita between 1932 and 1999.
     </p>
     <p>
      The report authors observed that, as metals are inherently recyclable, the metal stocks in society can serve as huge mines above ground (the term "
      <b>
       urban mining
      </b>
      " has been coined with this idea in mind
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      ). However, they found that the recycling rates of many metals are very low. The report warned that the recycling rates of some rare metals used in applications such as mobile phones, battery packs for hybrid cars and fuel cells, are so low that unless future end-of-life recycling rates are dramatically stepped up these critical metals will become unavailable for use in modern technology.
     </p>
     <p>
      <br/>
     </p>
     <p>
      The military recycles some metals. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Navy" title="U.S. Navy">
       U.S. Navy
      </a>
      's Ship Disposal Program uses
      <a href="/wiki/Ship_breaking" title="Ship breaking">
       ship breaking
      </a>
      to reclaim the steel of old vessels. Ships may also be sunk to create an
      <a href="/wiki/Artificial_reef" title="Artificial reef">
       artificial reef
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">
       Uranium
      </a>
      is a very dense metal that has qualities superior to lead and
      <a href="/wiki/Titanium" title="Titanium">
       titanium
      </a>
      for many military and industrial uses. The
      <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">
       uranium
      </a>
      left over from processing it into
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">
       nuclear weapons
      </a>
      and fuel for
      <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactor" title="Nuclear reactor">
       nuclear reactors
      </a>
      is called
      <a href="/wiki/Depleted_uranium" title="Depleted uranium">
       depleted uranium
      </a>
      , and it is used by all branches of the U.S. military use for armour-piercing shells and shielding.
     </p>
     <p>
      The construction industry may recycle concrete and old road surface pavement, selling their waste materials for profit.
     </p>
     <p>
      Some industries, like the
      <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">
       renewable energy
      </a>
      industry and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Solar_photovoltaic" title="Solar photovoltaic">
       solar photovoltaic
      </a>
      technology in particular, are being proactive in setting up recycling policies even before there is considerable volume to their waste streams, anticipating future demand during their rapid growth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Recycling of plastics is more difficult, as most programs are not able to reach the necessary level of quality. Recycling of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PVC" title="PVC">
       PVC
      </a>
      often results in
      <a href="/wiki/Downcycling" title="Downcycling">
       downcycling
      </a>
      of the material, which means only products of lower quality standard can be made with the recycled material. A new approach which allows an equal level of quality is the
      <a href="/wiki/Vinyloop" title="Vinyloop">
       Vinyloop
      </a>
      process. It was used after the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Olympics_2012" title="London Olympics 2012">
       London Olympics 2012
      </a>
      to fulfill the PVC Policy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="E-waste_recycling">
       E-waste recycling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: E-waste recycling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Computer_recycling" title="Computer recycling">
       Computer recycling
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RetiredCPUs.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1828" data-file-width="2792" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RetiredCPUs.jpg/220px-RetiredCPUs.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RetiredCPUs.jpg/330px-RetiredCPUs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/RetiredCPUs.jpg/440px-RetiredCPUs.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RetiredCPUs.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Microprocessors retrieved from waste stream
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/E-waste" title="E-waste">
       E-waste
      </a>
      is a growing problem, accounting for 20-50 million metric tons of global waste per year according to the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">
       EPA
      </a>
      . It is also the fastest growing waste stream in the EU.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kinver_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Kinver-17">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many recyclers do not recycle e-waste responsibly. After the cargo barge
      <a href="/wiki/Khian_Sea_waste_disposal_incident" title="Khian Sea waste disposal incident">
       Khian Sea
      </a>
      dumped 14,000 metric tons of toxic ash in
      <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">
       Haiti
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Basel_Convention" title="Basel Convention">
       Basel Convention
      </a>
      was formed to stem the flow of hazardous substances into poorer countries. They created the
      <a href="/wiki/E-Stewards" title="E-Stewards">
       e-Stewards
      </a>
      certification to ensure that recyclers are held to the highest standards for environmental responsibility and to help consumers identify responsible recyclers. This works alongside other prominent legislation, such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Waste_Electrical_and_Electronic_Equipment_Directive" title="Waste Electrical and Electronic Equipment Directive">
       Waste Electrical and Electronic Equipment Directive
      </a>
      of the EU the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      National Computer Recycling Act, to prevent poisonous chemicals from entering waterways and the atmosphere.
     </p>
     <p>
      In the recycling process, television sets, monitors, cell phones and computers are typically tested for reuse and repaired. If broken, they may be disassembled for parts still having high value if labor is cheap enough. Other e-waste is shredded to pieces roughly 10 centimetres (3.9 in) in size, and manually checked to separate out toxic batteries and
      <a href="/wiki/Capacitor" title="Capacitor">
       capacitors
      </a>
      which contain poisonous metals. The remaining pieces are further shredded to 10 millimetres (0.39 in) particles and passed under a magnet to remove ferrous metals. An
      <a href="/wiki/Eddy_current" title="Eddy current">
       eddy current
      </a>
      ejects non-ferrous metals, which are sorted by density either by a centrifuge or vibrating plates. Precious metals can be dissolved in acid, sorted, and smelted into ingots. The remaining glass and plastic fractions are separated by density and sold to re-processors. Television sets and monitors must be manually disassembled to remove lead from CRTs or the mercury backlight from LCDs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Plastic_recycling">
       Plastic recycling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Plastic recycling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Plastic_recycling" title="Plastic recycling">
       Plastic recycling
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="2448" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg/220px-Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg/330px-Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg/440px-Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spoon_recycling_3d_printing.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A container for recycling used plastic spoons into material for 3D printing
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Plastic recycling is the process of recovering scrap or waste plastic and reprocessing the material into useful products, sometimes completely different in form from their original state. For instance, this could mean melting down soft drink bottles and then casting them as plastic chairs and tables.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_recycling">
       Physical recycling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Physical recycling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Some plastics are remelted to form new plastic objects; for example, PET water bottles can be converted into polyester destined for clothing. A disadvantage of this type of recycling is that the molecular weight of the polymer can change further and the levels of unwanted substances in the plastic can increase with each remelt.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_recycling">
       Chemical recycling
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Chemical recycling">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      For some polymers, it is possible to convert them back into monomers, for example PET can be treated with an alcohol and a catalyst to form a dialkyl terephthalate. The terephthalate diester can be used with ethylene glycol to form a new polyester polymer, thus making it possible to use the pure polymer again.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Waste_plastic_pyrolysis_to_fuel_oil">
       Waste plastic pyrolysis to fuel oil
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Waste plastic pyrolysis to fuel oil">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Another process involves conversion of assorted polymers into petroleum by a much less precise thermal
      <a href="/wiki/Depolymerization" title="Depolymerization">
       depolymerization
      </a>
      process. Such a process would be able to accept almost any polymer or mix of polymers, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermoset" title="Thermoset">
       thermoset
      </a>
      materials such as vulcanized rubber tires and the
      <a href="/wiki/Biopolymer" title="Biopolymer">
       biopolymers
      </a>
      in feathers and other agricultural waste. Like natural petroleum, the chemicals produced can be used as fuels or as feedstock. A RESEM Technology
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      plant of this type in
      <a href="/wiki/Carthage,_Missouri" title="Carthage, Missouri">
       Carthage, Missouri
      </a>
      , USA, uses turkey waste as input material. Gasification is a similar process, but is not technically recycling since polymers are not likely to become the result. Plastic Pyrolysis can convert petroleum based waste streams such as plastics into quality fuels, carbons. Given below is the list of suitable plastic raw materials for
      <a href="/wiki/Pyrolysis" title="Pyrolysis">
       pyrolysis
      </a>
      :
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Mixed plastic (
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/HDPE" title="HDPE">
        HDPE
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LDPE" title="LDPE">
        LDPE
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene" title="Polyethylene">
        PE
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Polypropylene" title="Polypropylene">
        PP
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Nylon" title="Nylon">
        Nylon
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teflon" title="Teflon">
        Teflon
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Polystyrene" title="Polystyrene">
        PS
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ABS_plastic" title="ABS plastic">
        ABS
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Fibre-reinforced_plastic" title="Fibre-reinforced plastic">
        FRP
       </a>
       , etc.)
      </li>
      <li>
       Mixed waste plastic from waste paper mill
      </li>
      <li>
       Multi-layered plastic
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recycling_codes">
       Recycling codes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Recycling codes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Recycling_codes" title="Recycling codes">
       Recycling codes
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1875" data-file-width="2776" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg/220px-Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg/330px-Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg/440px-Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Recycling_codes_on_products.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Recycling codes on products
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In order to meet recyclers' needs while providing manufacturers a consistent, uniform system, a
      <a href="/wiki/Recycling_codes" title="Recycling codes">
       coding system
      </a>
      was developed. The recycling code for plastics was introduced in 1988 by the plastics industry through the
      <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Plastics_Industry" title="Society of the Plastics Industry">
       Society of the Plastics Industry
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Because municipal recycling programs traditionally have targeted packaging—primarily bottles and containers—the
      <a href="/wiki/Resin_identification_code" title="Resin identification code">
       resin coding system
      </a>
      offered a means of identifying the resin content of bottles and containers commonly found in the residential waste stream.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Plastic products are printed with numbers 1–7 depending on the type of resin. Type 1 (
      <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">
       polyethylene terephthalate
      </a>
      ) is commonly found in soft drink and water bottles. Type 2 (high-density polyethylene) is found in most hard plastics such as milk jugs, laundry detergent bottles and some dishware. Type 3 (polyvinyl chloride) includes items such as shampoo bottles, shower curtains,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hula_hoops" title="Hula hoops">
       hula hoops
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Credit_cards" title="Credit cards">
       credit cards
      </a>
      , wire jacketing, medical equipment, siding and piping. Type 4 (low-density polyethylene) is found in shopping bags, squeezable bottles, tote bags, clothing, furniture and carpet. Type 5 is
      <a href="/wiki/Polypropylene" title="Polypropylene">
       polypropylene
      </a>
      and makes up syrup bottles, straws,
      <a href="/wiki/Tupperware" title="Tupperware">
       Tupperware
      </a>
      and some automotive parts. Type 6 is
      <a href="/wiki/Polystyrene" title="Polystyrene">
       polystyrene
      </a>
      and makes up meat trays, egg cartons, clamshell containers and compact disc cases. Type 7 includes all other plastics such as bulletproof materials, 3- and 5-gallon water bottles and sunglasses.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Having a recycling code or the chasing arrows logo on a material is not an automatic indicator that a material is recyclable but rather an explanation of what the material is. Types 1 and 2 are the most commonly recycled.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">
       Economic impact
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Economic impact">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Critics dispute the net economic and environmental benefits of recycling over its costs, and suggest that proponents of recycling often make matters worse and suffer from
      <a href="/wiki/Confirmation_bias" title="Confirmation bias">
       confirmation bias
      </a>
      . Specifically, critics argue that the costs and energy used in collection and transportation detract from (and outweigh) the costs and energy saved in the production process; also that the jobs produced by the recycling industry can be a poor trade for the jobs lost in logging, mining, and other industries associated with production; and that materials such as paper pulp can only be recycled a few times before material degradation prevents further recycling.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=National_Waste_and_Recycling_Association&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="National Waste and Recycling Association (page does not exist)">
       National Waste and Recycling Association
      </a>
      (NWRA), reported in May 2015, that recycling and waste made a $6.7 billion economic impact in Ohio, U.S., and employed 14,000 people.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cost.E2.80.93benefit_analysis">
       Cost–benefit analysis
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Recycling&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Cost–benefit analysis">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <table class="wikitable" style="float:right; font-size:85%; margin-left:1em;">
      <caption>
       Environmental effects of recycling
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
        <a href="#cite_note-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </caption>
      <tr>
       <th>
        Material
       </th>
       <th>
        Energy savings
       </th>
       <th>
        Air pollution savings
       </th>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td>
        <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_recycling" title="Aluminium recycling">
         Aluminium
        </a>
       </td>
       <td>
        95%
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-15">
         <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-economistrecycle_13-1">
         <a href="#cite_note-economistrecycle-13">
          [13]
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
       <td>
        95%
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gar_2-16">
         <a href="#cite_note-gar-2">
          [2]
         </a>
        </sup>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WasteOnline_59-0">

         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>

       </td>
       <td>
        —
       </td>
      </tr>

       </td>

        20%
       </td>
      </tr>
